The types of fractures are cracks, and their figures are diverse, and the causes are distinct. For some fractures, they are caused by the impact of a blow, where the blow breaks the bone completely at the fracture site, leaving the bone as if split in wood, and for this reason, this type is named "ascial"; or the blow affects some part of the bone, cutting through the flesh but not reaching the marrow; and this type of fracture is called "levatio absoluta.
And the pain of these two fractures is great or small, and of a fracture that grates or rubs; its cause is percussion upon the foot or setting in place.
And this fracture is penetrating, nearly reaching the periosteum which covers the bone; or it is in the face of the bone, and the pain of these two fractures is great or small.
And of the fracture, it is weakened in the bone, in the subtlety of the small hair-like structure, and for this reason, this species of fracture is called capillary.
And this fracture is caused by setting or percussion of the foot, similar to which, it causes some lamina of the bone to enter the internal parts, and it occurs in the concave area, as happens with clay pots when they are percussed, and it is a part of their breaking into the internal parts.
And more commonly, this occurs in the bodies of moist bones, such as those of infants.
And in all these species of closed fractures, they are thus treated as if by separate stars. And I will come to the recollection of all this in its place.
And we know all these species of fracture by detection of them and by inquiry of their appearance and the corruption of flesh that is upon them.
And the capillary species is known because it is discovered upon the bone, is cleaned, and limits upon it by touch, for the fracture appears black.
More in the cure of fractures consider at the beginning the signs of illness, and if you see by these signs that it signifies a manifest indication of fear, such as vomiting of colic, extension, loss of understanding, suppression of voice, fainting, acute fever, swelling of the extremities and heat of those areas, and signs similar to them in major dispositions, then you should doubt that it is not healed.
And if you see uncommon signs, you hope for healing, and then you begin the cure of it.
When the injury has come to you at the beginning as an injury and this is during the days of winter, then when you study in the bone's membrane before the fourteenth day according to all disposition.
And if it is during the days of summer, then when you examine the bone before the seventh day, have in mind that what is left of the bone may be corrupted by putrefaction, and that accidents which we have recounted may occur.
And if the fracture has already reached the putrefaction covering the marrow, and is broken into many pieces, then you should incise the fractured part and rub it, according to what I will recount to you.
And when you incise the thigh of the injured patient, uncover over the bone according to custom and your method as is possible based on the figure of the injury, and let it be light over the wound.
And if you encounter difficulty in detecting the bone, flows of pus or abscesses, then you should apply pressure to it as is appropriate, and fill the area with cloths soaked in vinegar and rubbed oil, and let the wound rest until the abscess is subdued, and be sure of the flow of pus; and then begin at the circumference of the bone and in its excision.
And this should be according to one of two ways of operation.
And one way is that you incise the tissue straight down, and this is its form: Then administer another incisor here, a little further laterally; and this is its form:
And administer more with the incisor further laterally than the second.
And ensure that you have a number of different incisors, some of which are more lateral than others; and some of them are shorter and should be at the rear of their extremities in sharpness, and made of iron or steel.
And use them with ease in percussion on the incisors, so that the head does not tremble and guide it.
And if the tissue is strong and hard, then you must perforate in its circumference before administering the incisors with the superficial cuts, which you call shallow cuts.
And thus they are named so only when those do not pause at the end of the bone where it is close to it, for so that during travel there is a round extremity or margin above that upon which the head of the bone is left acute resembling a small margin or circle, which you must prohibit from being submerged and passing through the thickness of the bone.
And it is convenient that you take of these travels a great number of which each suits the thickness of each bone, ensuring that when present in all parts of the skull, the extremity of which is acute, the quantity in length and brevity be according to the thickness of this bone.
And this is the form of three species of travels: The quality of perforating in the circuit of the broken bone is that you place the travel on the bone and rotate it within those tongs until you know that the bone has already been penetrated; then, return the travel to another location, placing there what was between them to carry the quantity of the size of the ray or around that.
And then incise with the incisore in that which is between each part of the bone, and make that pus subtle that you can over it at the end of levity, as we have said, while excavating the bone, or with your hand, or with some other cause by certain instruments which you will apply to it, such as fine tongs and forceps.
And make sure that the scalpel does not touch any part of the chest.
And since you have circled the perforated bone, and separated it from the chest if it is continuous with it, and have extracted that part, make sure to scrape and smooth that area with a roughness similar to that of the bone but finer than other scalpel blades.
And if you find any small part of the ribs displaced, then reduce it using the tool we have mentioned for the instruments; and subsequently apply a salve to the wound and dress the area as we have said.
And the other method of operation is lighter, far from fear, which Diyssec Gualia and cuts it with great caution; this is his saying: At the beginning, you must begin by uncovering the part of the bone at the location where the fracture is most severe and most obvious. When you have uncovered that part, pause and use the end of this scalpel, which is slow and cheerful in its form:
And that the lenticular part is lion and does not incide any cause, and the acute part is divided into two halves lengthwise, as you see; that the lenticular part be adjusted to the cusp at the face of it incident acutely on the bone.
And then strike on the incisor of a part with a small hammer between which you have incised the whole bone as it is rotated, and you be sure of the panniculus that it does not accede in the accident that you fear in any manner, and if the medicant14 was the purest leaf of all men and the most ignorant of them; certainly, and if he were asleep.
And if any additional cause affects the bone of the panniculus in some places of the bone, incise that part at the extremity of the incisor lenticular, and deliver that part which is facilitated, for it is delivered from its noxious and fearful aspects.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate into the panniculus, and what is abscessed or left of the bone is its face, and remains in it asperities and subtle fibrils, then scrape that asperity and eradicate those fibrils with sharp instruments which you have prepared in various quantities so that you can administer from them the most convenient parts for that broken bone and the figure of it.
And ensure that you apply with your reason the os at the beginning to the fat of those razors, and then use closely beneath it the thin pus, and do not cease doing this continuously until you reach administering the thick pus and the thin pus of all.
In any other small fractures or subtle fractures, ensure that you apply care according to what is lost for the correction of it.
And this is because it is not complicated for those who have studied that art. And place this on what we have written about in great fractures.
And if the bandage is torn when you extract the bone, then you should take a piece of cloth according to the size of the wound, and submerge that in olive oil rubbed with rose water, and place that over the wound opening; then take another piece of folded or triple-folded cloth, submerge it in wine and olive oil, and place that over the first piece. Do this gently on top of that without pressing too hard. Then apply a poultice of honey over that, and do not press it except with the amount that the wound has.
Leave that point for one or two days until you are sure of the abscess; then remove it and apply medicines to which superfluous dryness is needed, such as linen lint and err seed flour, powder of enisetum and aristology, and similar.
And you make of these medicines a powder that is finely sifted over the wound, such as being dry; generally, apply all medicine to which it cleanses and does not irritate.
And preserve it according to its virtue so that the wound in your cure be clean; do not let there be surdicia of ointment or other in it; nor allow virulence to aggregate in it; for when it is applied over the cervele (brain) cloth, it corrupts that and spoils it, and you will injure the patient with this malignant evil.
And you will injure the scalp of the head, as it is modified by the bone and especially when the cure of it is neglected, and there is blackness on the surface of it; and therefore consider whether injury to the patient has occurred as we have said the accidents, know that it is harmful indeed and do all possible to avoid it.
And if the blackness occurs only due to the medicine that is applied above, and it is caused by that medicine, then take part of honey and part of rubbed oil in equal parts, and line a cloth with those lines, and place it over the scab; and cure it with the species of healing that you deem appropriate until it is healed.
Capitol. iii.
For the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that it is not the nose that is broken, but two parts together with some superfluous tissue above them, or one of those few layers, because in the nose there are two bones. The part that remains is cartilaginous and not broken; hence, you do not injure it, but rather apply friction, tortuosity, and sinuosity.
But if one of the two parts is broken, then you must introduce the small bone into the nostril, and equalize that fracture inside with your finger, and externally with your thumb, until the nose returns to its natural shape. And this is done for your convenience, and ensure that you do not aggravate the pain in the sick person's thigh during your operation.
And if the fracture is in the upper part of the nose, and cannot reach there, then you should treat it at the end of the nasal bone where there is a swelling.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you make the likeness of this.
And apply this on the restoration of it on the first day of the fracture, if possible; otherwise, soon after the seventh or eighth day, you should rest the apostema with a cautery.
And insert a piece of linen cloth at the end of the nose, if the fracture is in one part, or two slips of leather, if the fracture is in two parts, and ensure that the slip of leather has such a swelling as to fill the nostril.
And some of the ancient restorers say that the leather strips should be impregnated with balsam, and used throughout the day. But this is not what I see; instead, you should ensure that the cloth is soaked in balsam of Hungary and powdered alum; and leave it in place until the bone is set and the cartilage becomes hardened.
And sometimes gout is in the nose, instead of the foot, due to cold wrapping around with lion's skin cloth; and you have already fractured the nose very severely so that breathing is not prohibited.
And this cause is not necessary to you; and if you wish, do this, and if you wish, do gout.
And if another abscess occurs, cauterize it, apply a plaster to the nose with cerate or with a hot cloth in water and olive oil, or with a diaphyllum.
But if no abscess occurs, when you need to apply a plaster externally use sesame meal and powder of crushed cinnamon in honey; then place your hand on the patient's lion skin, and do not touch the nose for any reason.
But if the nose bones are fractured or comminuted, when you need to split those, extract them with instruments suitable for those; then close the incision, and treat it with what encases and consolidates, and with plasters appropriate for that.
If a plague occurs inside the nose, then cure that with cold waters and administer lead cans (a type of nasal irrigation) when they are all healed.
Chapter IV.
For the restoration of the jaw when it is broken.
When the jaw is broken and the fracture is not infected, consider whether the fracture is only superficial, not completely fractured into two parts, but bent inward in places. In this case, the pain is mild.
And therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, insert the left index finger into the patient's mouth; and similarly, if the fracture is on the left jaw, then insert the right index finger. Push the gelatinous substance of the fracture inward with ease towards the parts outside, using your other hand on the part outside of the bone; do this gently until it is in place.
And if the fracture of the jaw is already separated into two parts, then you should administer extension to both parts according to the correct alignment, so that their equilibrium can be restored.
And if you encounter a toothache with a fracture, then strengthen those of which you fear will break along the line of gold or silver or lead; and place a cerot on the broken jaw, and place a cloth duplicate, and a large star-shaped bandage properly made, or a piece equal to the length of the jaw; and lift it above according to how the wound is dressed and what you consider appropriate for application, provided that it is not destroyed.
And command the patient to rest and be tranquil, and place a lion's flesh broth on his wound.
And if you think that the figure has already changed in any way, then on the seventh day, you should remove it. Then correct what has already changed in him and plaster that area with powder of wheat, chalk of alum, or flour of cinnamon, after you have removed the cerot from him, and place lion's wool over the plaster.
And then, once this plaster is properly applied and the bone's position has not changed, leave it as it is, without removing it until he is healed, and strengthen the fracture; for marks of this fracture will close in three weeks.
And if another time a boil occurs, then administer what we have said repeatedly in the treatment of it until it is healed, and cure that boil.
And if the fracture is open, then consider whether it is a bad separation of the bone or displaced, then you carefully adjust those displacements as needed for the removal of those from the bones.
And if the openings of the wound are stretched, then you enlarge them with the flebotomy according to the quantity of your need.
And then, when you have adjusted those displacements and they will no longer be reduced, enlarge the openings of the wound if it is large; otherwise, place over that one of the plasters that suit and cover it, until it is healed.
Chapter V.
On the restoration of the furcula when it is broken.
Repeatedly, when the furcula is broken in the front part, the fracture is made in one of three ways.
If it is broken, or separated, or split into two parts without reaching the joints; and it is easy to restore; or as when accidents occur in the joints, and restoration is difficult; or as when the fracture is open.
And the operation in that case, when the fracture is open, is to have two assistants present, so that one holds the aid and follows the broken furcula, and the other extends the leg with that part.
Then adjust the fracture with the pins called entry points so that the shape of the bone is restored according to its natural form, and do not leave it in a depression or concavity.
And if more extension is needed, then take a pin or wick made of cloth or wool, of the size according to your need; extend and elevate the furcula and hold the pin between your fingers while adjusting the fracture as required.
And if you cannot pull the end of the furcula to the outer parts because it is caught in the preon, then you should turn the foot over its ankle, and place a medium-sized cushion on the muscle.
And the minister presses the muscle of the patient at the parts left between that of the furcula bone be elevated as in the presacral region, and then you align the fracture and set it at the sites said.
And if you feel the branches of the furcula and they are tender, then cut there and remove those branches with ease.
And if it is retained in the bone, then you make an incision in it with one of the scalpel blades which you have prepared for this, after placing your hand on the furcula to hold the cautery; and that cautery is made of wood or iron, of which this is the shape:
And it is similar to a spoon, of which this is the concavity; and its width should be according to what you need of it, according to the size of the bone or its smallness.
And the length of it should be according to what is possible for the operation; it have two ends thus as you see, and one end should be broad and the other narrow.
And if the wound rupture which you have incised when you have extracted the suture from the bone is large, and you are certain of an abscess, then you adjust the two edges of the wound with suturing.
And if the wound rupture is small, or you fear an abscess, then you use the wound with cloths and powders according to the size of the incision in the wound.
And if you encounter an abscess, then stuff a cloth with vinegar and rose oil, and place it over it.
And altogether, provided that the splint is not too tight over the broken bone; so that you can examine the wound daily, when the dressing becomes soft or see the splint has loosened, then adjust it and strengthen the ligament.
And place the patient to sleep with the head elevated on a pillow, and place another cushion under the neck so that when he sleeps at night it is slightly raised. This raises the support for the injured area, and thus elevates the fracture of the furcula along with the elevation of the muscle; or the arm hangs down by the side of the patient.
And do not remove the dressing unless there is an abscess or suppuration in the same place within twelve days.
And renew the poultice if you see that it is changing, and adjust the support and restore it, allowing the fracture of the furcula to be fully restored and coagulated.
And it is strengthened and comforted according to the case in twenty-eight days, and sometimes in some cases it is done in less time.
Capitol. VI.
For the restoration of shoulder fractures.
Shoulder blade is broken in the middle of the location and not broken of it but the edges.
And so when the location is broken — and it is broken in the middle of its location — and is not known otherwise than by touch, then according to the figure of the fracture, prepare it and reduce it according to the natural figure in every manner that is possible for you.
And then place over the area powder of ground honey and stop parsley; and then place over the wound a thin slice of table salt according to the size of the spoonful, or somewhat more.
More if you leave the slice concave in some part of the spoon, then equalize that concavity with stop parsley, and then place the slice according to equality.
And then strengthen over the area with a well-made ligature and ensure that the slice is firmly in place without being displaced from its location.
And consider the string in each day; and every time that it is softened, tie it again tightly and equally if it has been displaced from its place.
And fix the patient on his side.
And the spatula is restored in xx days or in .xxv.
And when this number is completed, then you dissolve the string, and know for sure that it belongs to the bones whose beginning was not feared nor their dissolution.
And if you proceed from the location of the bone to the ligament, then cut over it, and clamp it, and do as we have said in the fracture of the scapula with an apostema caut if this happens here.
Capitol vii.
On the restoration of the fracture of the foot.
The foot sometimes breaks in the middle of its location; however, its ends move and tend to become cicatrized and comminuted.
And concerning the accidents when his thigh is broken it declines to the inner parts and happens great pain, difficulty in walking, cough, fever and much vomiting; and it is declared in the cavity of the broken bone and is not hidden from sensation.
And the restoration of the patient is that he be laid supine on the surgeon's lap, and a cushion is placed between the patient's shoulders; then press on the muscles of the patient, adjust the patient's back against the major bones of the spine, and handle with subtlety in the operation and equate according to every manner that is possible and convenient, until the figure of the bone returns as it should.
And then place over the plaster and padding, and place above the thin table of willow or caligine and like them in lightness, since you wrap that in cloth; then handle with subtlety that over the broken bone so it is not rough; and wind it around the back several times, and strengthen it with a decent tension; and consider the bandage at all times, and when it becomes soft, strengthen it.
And if necessity provokes the death of that part due to corruption or puncture, or pain, or abscess that occurred in the place, then you cut and separate that part and eradicate the plaster and correct what occurred by this method of healing; then return the plaster, if you find it fit for that face; and adjust the structure between which it is healed.
Capitol VIII.
For the restoration of the ribs when they are fractured.
Know that a fracture of the ribs affects only large areas that connect them in pairs; and at the extremities of their front part, no contact occurs except by those parts which are cartilaginous. And the knowledge of this is not hidden from the feeling of the inquiry into it by words.
And the restoration should be such that you equalize the fracture with your hands according to the possible method, while it is reduced as it fits, and then apply plaster and strengthen the broken bone with a splint, if needed.
And if the fracture of the ribs is declining towards the interior parts, then it happens that severe pain and puncture occur in the patient, just as pain occurs in one who has pleurisy, and for this reason let him have a veil placed; and it causes him difficulty in breathing, coughing, and vomiting of blood; and this is of difficult cure.
And already the Ancients were ingenious in such matters.
And according to them: place the patient on a soft bed that causes inflammation and swelling, so that the belly becomes swollen and extended, and the fracture is pushed towards the exterior parts.
But we advise this so that it is not a trivial cause for generating a carbuncle inadvertently; and if it does happen, then help the patient.
And some of them say that it should be placed above the abdomen and near where it is strong; and this seems reasonable, but it is feared that the abdomen might draw the superfluities to that place due to its weakness.
And some of them would say: cover the area with wool that is already submerged in caut oil, and place powders between the ribs until it is filled, making sure the dressing is equal when you wrap it according to roundness; and then treat the patient with a pleursy diet and medical treatment.
And if the pain is intense and the patient cannot tolerate it, and you puncture the skin with a lancet, be careful about the patient, then cut over the area, expose the broken rib, and place your hand over that instrument which will conserve the chip, the memory of which precedes, and carefully incise the bone, and extract it.
And when we adjust the two edges of the wound, if it is large, with suturing, and treat it with ointments until it is healed.
And if an abscess occurs inside, then incise and insert powders in diluted oil, place your hand over the area, and treat the patient with what is appropriate for the abscess inside.
And may he be laid on his back on the one on which he sleeps, until he is healed.
Capitol. ix.
For the restoration of fracture of the vertebra of the back and the neck.
In some cases, when there is a fracture of the bones of the neck, and then apply friction, similar to that of the vertebrae of the back. And when this happens to someone and you want to know if they will be healed or not, then consider whether their veins are already softened, numbed, and dead, unable to increase or extend or strengthen those areas. When you compress them with your hands or prick them with a needle and do not feel any pain in them, then you know that they are not healing, and under such conditions, it is fatal.
And if you feel pain in these areas when you compress them with your hands and prick them, then you know that the vertebra of the bone is saved and that the patient will recover with treatment.
And if you see the vertebrae of the neck closely and want to know if they are healthy or not, then examine his feet.
And when you see that they are already softened, attend to what happens in the bowels, and when it is close to their anus, show windiness and desire for urination, and sometimes they are supine on their back and want to urinate but cannot, then know that it is mortal, and then do not trouble with the cure of them.
But if no such causes occur, the cause is better and milder.
And the cure for them is that you should cauterize to induce sedation of the abscess. Place the cautery on the lumbar vertebra or slightly rubbed spine; place it over that area for some days each day until the abscess is sedated.
And then place one of the comforting and existing plasters, and apply it over that area. Order the patient to rest and be tranquil, and not to sleep on the part where he feels pain until he is healed.
If you find that the joint is dislocated or due to some cause that has separated from it, then you should treat the joint and hold that bone in place; then adjust the two edges of the wound, and suture if it is large; afterwards, apply a reddening ointment until it heals.
If the lower end of the thigh bone is broken, and it is the end of the femur, then you should insert your left hand's little finger into the buttock, and align the broken thigh bone with your other hand as much as possible for second-order reduction; then place a compress and bandage over it if needed, and firmly hold it in place.
If you feel dislocated joints in it, then treat that joint, set it in place, and cure the wound with the treatment that follows until it heals.
Capitol.
For the restoration of the ankle.
The bones of the ankles are broken, and if they break, then the fracture is merely communication at the extremities of those bones and they split lengthwise and decline inward; pain occurs in the area and there is puncture, and the leg becomes numbed when it fractures.
And the restoration of it is that you place your hand over that area which is at the fracture where its shape is.
And if the fracture is only in the extremity of the limb, then you equalize it according to what appears to you by equilibrium, placing it so that it resembles its natural figure.
But if the fracture is in the middle or already declining towards the parts, then you lay the limb on its side with its abdomen prepared for the restoration of that fracture. And when you have equalized it, apply a plaster over it, and place a wick or stick astelia over that area, and bind it tightly so that you fear neither the puncture of the fracture nor the removal of the stick.
And equalize the cavity with what fills it so that it takes the binding according to equality, and command the patient to lie on his back or on his side.
But if an abscess forms, then you cease the extension of it and its restoration until the abscess is sedated, and apply something to soothe it as previously instructed; and then return to its restoration, and bind it accordingly.
And if accidents occur in the bones or are fractured, then you treat them.
Capitol. xi.
For the restoration of the fracture of theadjuctor.
Adjutori is that which is between the codex and the head of the shoulder.
And if it is broken, then the restoration of it is in one of two ways, of which one you should use a rod of rough wood according to this form:
And attach two strings at the ends, and then suspend them from the elevated location.
And place the patient on a cushion; and then hang out his broken arm on the rod until it reaches the nodal point of which the curvature of the rod is attached.
And place it suspended above something heavy, or the servant extend it to the parts left; and then the doctor hold the fracture with his hands at the same time, until the fracture returns according to its natural course.
And another way is that the patient be supine on his neck and suspend the neck with a liament; then command two ministers that one of them hold what is above the fracture with his hands; and the other hold the rest of him; and each of them extend to their part.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, then strengthen the area above the fracture with one liament and another liament at the other place, and each of the two ministers extend to their parts.
And therefore, if the fracture is near the extremity of the muscle, then place the middle of the liament over the tendon and let the other end be at the fracture to hold it.
And similarly, if the fracture is near the joint, then place the liament at that location and over the joint.
Equalize that with ease and your work, and then strengthen it when it is equalized according to what is needed and applied properly.
But if an abscess occurs on the fifth day, then leave the string in place, place a pad of wool soaked in wine and olive oil under it, and when the abscess relaxes, then remove that.
The manner of removing it is to place a poultice over the fracture, and then wrap a cloth around the poultice; then adjust the arm on this support, and place your hand open over its muscle.
And place the cloth and firmly hold the arm on this support so that the arm is in a stable position, unless some cause of the broken bone prevents you from doing so.
And then use stays (or splints).
And you place that stay over the fracture which is both wider and stronger than the other stays; and between each stay, you leave the width of a digit, and the length of the stays according to the breakage plus the width of three digits in total.
And then firmly hold the stays with the string which I mentioned at the beginning of this chapter.
And it is that your pressure on the place of the fracture should be more straight. And when the fracture is longer, make the pressure less.
And so, if you place the position of the bones and apply pressure as we have said when restoring the member to its position, then you do this.
And if you fear an abscess, wait, then leave the pressure and bones in place as we have said.
And then consider the ligament in all parts for three days so that no infection or inflammation occurs; or is forbidden food to reach the member due to the excess of pressure; and so correct all this as we have said.
But if you are confident that no cause of this will occur, then do not release the bandage for at least three days or more.
And let the patient lie on their back, and their hands be over their stomach.
And place yourself over the assistant who is holding a woolen blanket, and consider at all times of the night or day that the figure of the broken limb should not be altered nor the ligament softened, therefore correct this entirely with your virtue.
And place the food for the patient according to the order we have mentioned, ensuring that the food starts subtle and gradually strengthens; then cover to thicken the food with it.
Four days after, it is customary for the assistant and the belt to be strengthened for 10 days; then cover to make them loose, and apply baths and ointments which correct that.
And if the fracture has been irritated, then do not make them loose of the ligament and the stitches for 10 days or up to two months.
Capitol.xii.
On the restoration of fracture of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, which are called the ulna and the radius; the former is small, and is that which extends from the thumb side, while the latter is large and is placed next to the small one.
And if the large or small fire stick is broken forcefully, and you must break them together forcefully.
And when the small fire stick is broken above, then the restoration of it is light, and the healing of it is even lighter.
And when the fire stick is broken, I leave the fracture to be bad, and the healing of it to be difficult.
And worse than that is when two common bones are broken.
And so, if the bone that is broken is the small fire stick above, then it is convenient for the doctor to perform the restoration of it, allowing a small extension with facilitation until that which is broken is equalized.
But if the large fire stick is what is broken, then you must allow greater extension more vigorously.
And if both fire sticks are broken together, then you must allow greater extension more forcefully.
And you must place the figure of your hand for the restoration and extension of it on a cushion, with the wrist of the hand above, more so than all others, and the auricular finger below the other fingers, and the thigh be placed over a sloping bench.
And place a cushion in front of them at their head or side, so that the patient does not further strain.
And then the minister extends the arm of the affected side and places it either on his own hand or on a cushion, and another minister extends it over similarly.
And then the doctor aligns the bone within the joint according to the best shape it can be placed in.
And if there are fragments in the fracture of the bone, then you must force to reduce all pieces to their place with your virtue.
And if there appears a separated fragment that cannot be reduced to your satisfaction in the restoration of the bone, then you set it as best you can according to the manner we have said above.
More if the fracture is comminuted, I have dealt with that in a separate chapter, and therefore take up the treatment of that.
And if an abscess occurs at the beginning of your reduction, then you line a cloth with rosin oil and white wax, making it medium in thickness. Place the cloth firmly over the area with a subtle constriction to allow the abscess to drain; then remove the wax and apply an ointment prepared from powder of molybdenum and honey, and cover it with a bandage; let the bandage be placed on that fracture, slightly aside and more strongly.
And know that the number of the bones in the arm I saw in the major dispositions, or rather the fracture in one joint or in two joints together.
And then place your compression firmly on the location of the fracture more strongly and more vigorously, and the stronger the compression on the parts above the fracture or on the lower parts, place the compression a little more gentle according to how the memory of it is recalled at the beginning of the chapter.
And the cloths that you wrap around the fracture should be moist but not hard or rough, and the fat of the liver which is strengthened should be of refined meat between fine and coarse, as we have recommended.
And consider the limb and the ligament for several days. And if any cause arises for which correction is necessary, as you anticipate what may happen to the limb, then immerse the limb in water until that anticipation ceases.
And leave the limb not compressed until it rests, and then remove the compression.
And if the stricture is already softened and the bone is healed and resembles those, then correct all of this with your virtue.
And consider more if the nourishment is prohibited from reaching the member due to excessive stricture, so that you soften it a little and leave it for some days until nutrition flows to it, and then strengthen it.
And if to the patient none of the causes we have mentioned occur, then it is not necessary for it to be let loose within about ten days or around those.
And place the hand of the patient with its virtue of movement-agitating substances, and make it sleep on its back.
And know that this fracture is restored in thirty days or in thirty-two days, or more thoroughly in twenty-eight days, and all of this is according to the disposition of the complexions and the dispositions of the virtue.
Chapter XIII.
On the restoration of fractures of the hand and fingers.
Know that at the tip of the hand and the joints of the fingers there must be tenderness on palpation, and do not damage them but rub with visible motas.
And when there is damage to the hand or tearing, then make sure the patient stays lying down and in front of the limb for one hundred seconds according to equality, and place your hand over the extended area.
And then the minister extend the broken bones and the doctor adjust those between which there should be good alignment.
And then place plaster and bandage, if no abscess forms use a cautery instead; and then place salt over according to the size of the area, and you have now wrapped that in cloth.
More so if the tear is within the parts of the palm, then make a semblance of a hollow in the cloth, and command the patient to hold that with his torn palm; and then hold it tightly with long cloth.
And let the salt be placed on cloth that is soft, so that the salt is applied to the palm; and hold that tightly as needed.
If the fracture is on the outer parts, then you should place another piece of the bone above the stele and another piece from the part in the palm, so that your hand is open and straight; then place the splint, as you turn your hand like a wheel between the fingers with lint.
If a fracture occurs in one of the phalanges of the finger, then if it is the base of the digit, equalize it according to where it is and then strengthen it with the palm. If you wish, place a small splint on the right side so that the fracture is elevated and not displaced.
If the fracture is in the other fingers, as in the middle finger, or the index, or the ring, then equalize and bind it to the finger where that bone is; or bind all of them according to continuity, which is better; or place a small stele over it, as we said for the base of the digit.
And consider, in your restoration, and nearby, the placement of cauterization and opposition—during your operation—according to where it is when any cause from those occurs, as per the repeated narration of it.
Chapter XIV.
For the restoration of fracture of the iliac bone.
The iliac bone, when fractured, is felt according to where it is displaced anteriorly and posteriorly.
And the restoration is done by strengthening the ligament over the fracture and another ligament below the fracture, with the patient lying on their belly and facing downward.
And then the physician extends the ligament along its proper side according to the quality of the fracture, when it is in the middle part of the bone.
But if it is close to the base of the iliac bone, then the ligament should be extended along the outer side of the pubic bone, so that the extension fits there, and another ligament below the fracture.
And similarly, if the fracture is close to the pubic bone, then the ligament should be extended along the pubic bone, and the extension to the sides below.
And then the doctor aligns the bone by bringing his hands close to where you have reduced it according to the natural shape, and the bone is joined by good articulation; then apply a plaster and bandage, so as not to affect the limb with an abscess. But if an abscess does occur, then you let it be for a few days until the abscess can be cauterized, and then return to your treatment.
The bandaging should be done as follows: make sure to wrap a hard bandage around the fracture, fold it two or three times, and tie off any excess; then wrap the bandage until it reaches the sole and the heel of the foot.
And insert a long piece of linen between the thigh and the leg near the knee, so that the ends of the linen extend above both parts, and then wrap this around the leg and thigh with any excess from the bandage.
And place something over the thigh at the site of the fracture, and place a splint over the bone of the leg.
And then fill the void between the waist and the thigh with soft cloth, so that the constriction is constricted.
And then begin with a strong bandage around the middle over the location of the fracture using three or four wraps. The farther you are from the site of the fracture, the less tight your bandage should be, and it should be lighter and softer.
And then extend the ends of the bandage to the parts where it reaches the foot's cavity, and tightly secure both ends of the crosspieces of the other part so that the plaster does not shift from its place.
And then place the bandage over it so that it is secure and does not cause corrosion, abscess, or inflammation. If any of these conditions occur, you should treat them accordingly and correct whatever has happened as soon as possible, as we have previously described.
More specifically, if the bone is severely damaged, you should try to set it as accurately as possible; if not, apply a splint and secure it, and treat the area from which the memory of the injury was recalled until it is healed.
And sometimes this fracture of the hip is reinforced with strapping or that they fit well to the ilium, as we said in the assistant and in the arm.
But more so in the restoration of the leg in such a manner, no limp occurs to the patient; and if it is restored alone, or that the other leg is applied, then it is necessary for the other to limp.
And know that the hip is reinforced in about ten days or a few months, and all this is according to the diversity of the complications and other dispositions.

Know that the fracture of the tibia occurs more frequently, but it often takes many weeks to heal.

And consider all the dispositions which we have recommended in other fractures, thus as for abscesses, cauterization, and appearance to those that arise when one opposes them to any accident at what is suitable until it is healed.
Capitol. XVI.

Chapter XVII.
Of the fracture of the tarsus and the bones of the foot and toes.
Fractures do not occur in the tarsus as a whole; more often fractures affect the bones of the foot; and fractures of the toes of the foot occur rarely, and when they do, they are not treated by fracturing them but are rubbed in the appropriate manner.
And if fractures occur in the bones of the foot and sometimes those bones need to be elevated above each other, then place the patient's foot on a prepared surface as shown.
Then place the other foot on what is elevated beneath those bones, and compress, press, and align those bones back into place.
Then apply a plaster and cover it with a cloth. Place the patient's foot on a small table with two pillows under it.
Then firmly hold the foot in a proper position, as you have wrapped it with cloths and other causes mentioned.
And when three or four days have passed, then you untie the bandage, for you will find that the bones are broken or shattered or displaced, and the operation in those cases is as you know.
And if some bones are said to be broken, then restore those, and set them as I and have taught you in the restoration of the fingers of the hand. And place the fractured finger steadily according to its length, and slightly bend it; then lay the hand on this table which you have already prepared, and tightly bind it with a bandage.
And if what is broken of the bones are two or three or more, then you place each fractured finger individually, wrapping a quantity of it in lion skin, and strengthen the table at the base of the foot, and standing on one of the table's ends, pierce the sole of the foot to ensure it has good retention.
And it is advisable for you not to forget that you should consider all the causes we have mentioned in other fractures of the accidents we have discussed; and pause at every occurrence as appropriate.
Chapter XVIII.
Of the fracture of the thigh bone and the pelvis and the groin of the man.
When the thigh bone is fractured, make that correction in a curved position, and then slightly incline it towards the upper part of the leg by a little, and keep it stable behind.
And then the midwife fills the thigh with cloth, and this should be done in the thigh as desired; and then the woman is stimulated, and gradually raises her leg a little by little, for all of this must be done before the upper part of the thigh as desired and the fractured bone returns.
And then place powders on the leg, and when she wants to urinate, moisten the cloth to facilitate it; and then let her urinate, return to that position according to the method in which you placed it at the beginning, and then return to its original powder.
And do so for seven days or until it is restored.
And if you wish, take a bladder of sheep and stretch it over the wound in the thigh; and insert all the bladder into the wound, and then blow through the cannula with virtue until the bladder inflates inside the wound, because the fracture returns.
And it should be filled with pus and left for some days as we have advised, until it heals.
And when the bone of the thigh of a man or a woman is broken, then administer in the restoration and needle treatment of it what we have advised for the bone of the hip.
And what is right is not hidden in these nearby fractures and those that occur rarely, especially to him who has some study and use and understands this my free will; for that fracture means to you in the major disposals via recta in the restoration and in the constriction of it, and therefore understand well.
In the case of a man's thigh when broken, protect it from infection, and insert the thigh into it; then place a bandage around it, wrap it up, and leave it for three days or about that time, until it heals.

Chapter XXIII.
On Speech Regarding Dislocation.
Dislocation is an abnormal position of one of the joints in the body, rendering it motionless and altering the figure of the limb, causing severe pain and significant injuries to the patient.
Therefore, when a dislocation occurs, it is necessary to act promptly to reduce it. Do not delay the reduction, for if delayed, the joint will become septic and more difficult to reduce.
And for this reason, it should not be cut nor stretched during the hour of the aposteme, because moments after it causes spasms and severe pains.
When this happens, you should perform a femoral incision on the thigh, and allow the aposteme to rest inside; then apply cold water and oil to the affected area, and reduce it with ease.
And each affected member should be treated with water, the memory of which will return to its place.
And you have already arranged the chapters on dislocation according to what follows in fracture; from the upper part of the body down to the lower part.

On the treatment of dislocation of the jaw, proceed.
When both jaws are dislocated without damage; the dislocation of both can occur in one of two ways: either they are slightly moved from their places and somewhat softened; or they are completely dislocated and fully displaced, at which point they should be softened at the joint where saliva from the throat flows, so that neither can have that nor can close its mouth, and the tongue should be wrapped in a cloth.
When the dislocation is slight, it usually returns by itself in major positions.
But if the dislocation is complete and thorough, then it must be administered a slight reduction, and should not be delayed entirely.
And the minister should hold the patient's head, while the doctor inserts one of his fingers from the side of the mouth into the gap of the dislocated jaw, if the dislocation is on one side; or he should insert both of his fingers together, if the dislocation is on both sides, and keep the other fingers outside to support them. The doctor then commands the patient to stop moving his jaw and allows it to return to its place; the doctor then aligns the jaw and closes it gently until it returns to its position.

And this should be administered freely, and you should not interfere with the intake of food and drink until the jaw is moistened and healed.

Chapter XXVI.
On the reduction of the dislocation of the muscle.
Know that the muscle is dislocated in three ways, one of which is when it is dislocated from the tendon to the bone.
And the second is when it is dislocated from above the muscle, which is called "tart"; and it is not dislocated at the back parts through the shoulder; and it is not dislocated at the front parts through the nerves.
And it is most commonly dislocated and pushed outward at the back parts of the tendon where the flesh is scanty. It is slightly dislocated in those parts where there are more muscles, but with difficulty.
And often it happens to some men through percussion or impact, and the muscle of him is abscessed from a carbuncle, and is cut by this that already be dislocated.
Then touch where you were especially concerned with it, and then proceed in the cure of it.

If it is dislocated, you must touch the foot or ankle, and then you find a clear tremor, and it is not hidden.

Chapter XXVII.
On the treatment of dislocation of the shoulder.
Know that the shoulder joint is dislocated with difficulty and seems to return with difficulty; it can be dislocated in all directions, but most commonly in front and behind. The dislocation of the shoulder is not easily masked; whether you see it quickly or feel it by touch depending on the shape that is dislocated. When you set the dislocated shoulder into place, this is clearly manifest to you, for the joint is concave and cannot bend the arm or allow the hand to reach its own muscle.
And observe the shoulder blade, and reduce the dislocation as soon as possible before it comes to a carbuncle, for if it does come to a carbuncle, reduction is difficult, and even with strong force, it may not be completely healed. Especially when it is in the rear part, for it is more harmful than all other types of dislocation, causing greater pain, and often leads to death through warm baths.

And if the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then administer violent extension and strong force.
And let two ministers extend the arm, and have these two ministers hold it in place so that it is not forced too much during the extension.

And then release it; and if the joint is formed in that place, then you release the ligament and leave it.

Capitol. xxviii.
For the treatment of dislocation of the shoulder, it is the joint where the arm meets the hand.
Shoulder of the hand is sometimes dislocated, and the reduction of this dislocation is easy. The opposite is true for other joints; however, it is more important that there be speed in the reduction of it at the hour when it is dislocated, before the area becomes inflamed or an abscess occurs.
And the reduction of the shoulder dislocation is that you place the patient's hand joint on a table and instruct them to extend their arm. The doctor then places his hand or palm over the vicinity of the joint, and gently push it back into its proper position.
But importantly, it should be considered if the palm is already placed on the patient's abdomen, then the patient's hands should be laid out and reduced; and if dislocation is present, place both hands, ensuring the position of the hand precisely on the table so that the doctor's hand falls over that vicinity of the joint.
And so if it returns quickly, that is good; otherwise, strengthen that and drain the abscess, leaving that; and do not repeat this, for if you do, you will neither be able nor permitted to proceed with the reduction thereafter.
But importantly, the joint should be manipulated according to its tortuosity; and do not note any cause to the patient, but rather have your hand softened and cannot strengthen any cause; then you know that the nerves are compressed or stretched, and then there is no need in it but to cauterize near the site of injury and fortify it with some cause.
And since the abscess has returned, place it over the drained area, which we have previously treated, and let it be strengthened for five days; then, return it to its position, leave it there, and exercise the limb.
But if the motion in it has been obstructed and occurs with some gravity, then apply that which is cold and with friction several times, until it is alleviated.
Chapter XXIX.
Of the treatment of dislocation of the fingers.
In some cases, dislocate the fingers completely.
And when one finger is dislocated on the side of the palm or in its middle part, then hold the finger and push the dislocation back with your thumbs, until it returns to place.
Then hold the base of the finger, and suspend that part which is dislocated, leaving it for two days.
Then suspend that part and extend it until it is straightened in that day.
And when it is night, bind it according to that procedure, and do not cease to loosen it during the day and exercise with movement, and bind it at night; and do this for several days until it is straight.
And make it seem as if you do this, even if dislocated in the hand, bind that to that part and do with this your first operation until it is healed.
And make it seem as if you do this when dislocated in other parts.
Capitol.
For the cure of dislocation of the cervical spine.
When an entire vertebra of the back or neck is dislocated and multiple vertebrae are displaced from their original positions, then there is no cure, for death ensues to the patient.
And the sign of this is that the patient's excretion is forced out against his will, and he cannot retain it; several times muscles of the body or legs or arms or one of those are softened. Or a vertebra is displaced from its place, for multiple times a vertebra is displaced and multiple times the removal of those is done in four parts.
Then that is relieved at the rear parts and is named yellowness.
May the cure of it be that when you consider if the yellowness is due to puerperium, then in that case there is no cure and neither does it heal completely.
May it be that in those cases which come from conception or percussion or resemblance to those, the Ancients have already detailed the ways of cure in much discourse, of which the most returns to prophecy; but I will now abbreviate what is of little excess of the tropes, by my proximity to your understanding exposition, to which I have here expanded and formed from various instruments, with which I shall show and expound that.
And of those, only the one that occurs properly at the foot is cured as I recommend.
And the patient should be extended on his back close to the wall, and laid out so that his feet are well moistened to prevent them from drying.
And place a straight stick, set in the ground at the end of the back near its head, and another stick across the feet at the other end of the beam.
The minister should hold the stick, and it should not be fixed in the ground; another minister should hold the other end according to that form.
And wrap around the head of the patient and tie the lion's hide tightly. Extend the end of the hide to the stick which is fixed at the head of the patient, and attach it there; and tighten the other hide over the hips of the patient with the nails, and fix the legs of the patient; and adjust all the hides tightly and attach them to the other stick which is fixed at the feet of the patient.
And the minister should extend the hide to the part of the stick, and do not remove both sticks and the hides in which they are inserted, but rather keep them fixed as we have said.
And the doctor places his palms on the spine with strength until it returns, or he places one hand on a table and adjusts his feet on the table as it returns.
And if it does not return in this manner, then take a table whose width or length is about three braccia, and dig into the wall where we said was near the patient a place between the ends of the table. And place the majority of the table's length on the spine, and the doctor places his feet on the other end, and presses firmly so that the spine is compressed and returns to its place.
And if you wish, open a vein which is pulsating in the hand; and when you are on the ground, place the patient's head at the heel of one stick, whose length is that of a braccio, and place his head at the heel of another stick; and let the distance be such that the space between each stick is a palm. Then draw blood from both sticks, and secure each stick firmly to the ground so they do not move.
And there should be a round stick for the thigh, and it should be rotated in the two cuts of the two sticks.
And at the end of it is a stick on which the round stick is formed, the length of which is one palm in relation to what is rotated; and in the two other sticks, it is similar to that.
And then strengthen the ligaments you have stretched on the foot with the stick on which you place its head, and the ligaments you have stretched on the thighs with the thigh where you place its feet.
And then you should place each thigh and have a minister twist the thigh with his hand, and the doctor straighten the spine as we have said.
And when the spine is returned and the area is straightened, then cover it with a paste of honey, and some wool; place a piece of cloth over the paste, the width of which should be about three fingers or slightly more, and its length enough to cover the part of the spine.
And then apply the substance we have mentioned, and use the patient for subtle nourishment until it is healed.
And if there is any danger at the site at the end of the healing, then it is advisable to administer medicines that mitigate and relieve through continuous use as recommended on the table; and a lead plate has already been administered in that location.
Sometimes accidents occur at the end of the vertebrae, and it is seized by that dislocation; and it is that bone where you already anticipate that no suspicion should be caused to it with this medicine, for it will greatly happen to cause your death.
Capitulum XXXI.
On the treatment of dislocated hip.
Know that the joint of the hip and the joint of the muscle should occur only due to dislocation, and not what occurs in other joints due to slight movement and depression.
The joint of the hip is dislocated according to four manners, which is dislocated to the internal parts, or to the external parts, or to the anterior parts, or to the posterior parts.
It is primarily dislocated to the internal parts, and little to the anterior or posterior parts.
The sign of dislocation to the internal parts is that when you adjust the leg of the healthy side against the affected side, the former appears longer and the latter more prominent. The affected side cannot bend its foot; it must be lengthened. This is the location where a cautery should be applied to cauterize the apostema, so that the head of the swelling is effectively treated here.
The sign of those affected with dislocation to the external parts is that their symptoms are contrary to this condition.
The sign of dislocation to the anterior parts is that you expand the leg in proper proportion, but it cannot bend at the ankle. If forced to bend further, it causes pain. The urine from this side is retained, and the swelling is an apostema. You should treat its mobility over the tibia.
And the sign of those who suffer dislocation in the rear parts is that they do not expand the buttocks, nor can that which should bend forward bend it backward, and the waist is shorter than the other, and the buttock is soft at that place.
The main reduction in the species of this dislocation is that you consider, at that time, if the dislocation is old and already degenerated, that it has been there for some time. Then, according to its disposition, you perform a reduction on it as needed; but then there should be no presumption made in that treatment.
For the case where the dislocation is recent and one of the four species of dislocation, you should manipulate it so that you reduce the joint and extend it to both internal and external parts, and move it to the right and left sides, for it will strongly return on its own without requiring further treatment.
Moreover, if it does not return in this manner, you should prepare a strong medicine and extend the waist on the side more affected, or on their hands, or apply liniment which is rubbed on the waist above the buttocks; another medicine should extend the hand from above so that it touches the inner sides of their legs; then strongly apply liniment on the back of the thigh, and have another person hold the end of the liniment.
And it is the extension from the loins or from inside the iliac part to the axillary part, or from behind to the lumbar part.
And it is the extension of all those in one go, lifting the patient's body from the ground and letting them hang pendulously.
And this type of extension is special for all four.
And therefore, if the dislocation returns as we have said, it will be good; otherwise, for every type, what I say about its own treatment is necessary.
In the reduction of its own, when dislocated, and the dislocation is in the internal parts, ensure that the patient's body is supine on their left side; then place the bandages around the hip in that location where between the head of the hip and the location where the iliac part is placed. Additionally, extend the bandage from above the area of the axilla to the next part of the ilium.
And then another minister firmly grasp the thighs of the patient, and compress the large area of the affected hip, extending it outward forcefully; for this will cause the dislocation to return to its proper place.
And this species is of a milder cure for which that member is reduced compared to other species.
And if it is excused upon you and you do not comply with the training for this species of cure, then cover that patient's two feet together with firm but gentle bandaging, over both the ankles and both the insteps.
And let the length of each be by the quantity of four fingers, and let the patient's leg be extended more than the other by a quantity of two fingers.
And then the patient's leg should be悬挂于杆子的顶部，且应置于手中；其长度应为两臂长。
And then command a strong youth to hold down the thigh, and have another strong youth suspend the patient's leg, with the other youth holding down the thigh firmly, so that the joint returns to its place quickly.
And in the reduction proper, when dislocation is at the outward part, cover the patient's leg with a cloth according to what we have advised when he was brought in; and strengthen the bandaging over the patient's leg and its ankle.
And nearby two rods be placed, one on the foot and the other on the head; and nearby those rods adjust them firmly over the middle of the patient's thigh, upon which wet cloths be wrapped, so that the patient is not harmed because the rod is between their thighs, for you should allow the extension to the parts left undone.
And each minister extend to his part, and the doctor with his hand equalize the reduction according to the art.
And when the dislocation is in the front parts, then let the leg be extended slightly, and this is according to this narrative method for the thigh; and the doctor places the palm of his right hand on the patella of the diseased knee, and nearby tightens the other hand, and with that tightening place the extension at the part left to the kneecap.
And if the dislocation is at the back parts, then do not allow the patient to be stretched out on his back and elevated above the ground; instead, he should be placed on a hard surface, as we said when the wider part was dislocated towards the outer side, according to what we have said about the lying down of the patient on two widths; and his face and limbs should be extended, as we said.
And a compress should be applied on the place where the joint is dislocated.
Therefore, once the reduction is completed as it should — and you notice the sign of the reduction, which is when you extend both legs of the patient, and when you see them equal and the leg returns to its normal position by expanding that part, then you know that the limb has been returned according to what is required — then you place both pillows and apply bandages so that the width does not change in any part; and you keep the leg at rest for three or four days.
And then separate the legs and compare one thigh to the other.
And if you see equal marks in the wound, then you know that the dislocation is already healed, and then allow the patient to go.
And if you see any sign of softening, then return and apply a poultice to that area, and reposition it according to your original structure, and leave it for three days.
And then move that area to gradually improve mobility in each part over some days until it is comfortable.
Chapter XXXII.
On the treatment of dislocation of the shoulder.
The shoulder can be dislocated in three ways: it can be dislocated to the outward parts, to the inward parts, and to the front parts more than to the back parts; and it is not dislocated to the rear parts.
And the sign of shoulder dislocation is that when you command the patient to apply their thigh to their chest; then you know that the shoulder is dislocated.
And the restoration of all manners of the patient is that you make sure the thigh is straight, try to extend its leg as much as possible over this; attach your two hands to the face of it, and place his foot between your feet; then make sure the sole adheres to your palms, retain those between your toes on the sole of it with strength, and another attendant stretch his leg so that the sole returns to its place.
And the sign of relief for the patient is that his leg adheres to the thigh lightly without effort.
And then apply it and make the leg adhere to the thigh, and place those continuously with a bandage for three or four days, and then remove those.
And do not use the splint for ambulation for some days until it is healed and conforted.
If the reduction of it is excused to you, then administer strong extension to the limbs of which in the cure of the hip, a previous narration was given.
Capitol. xxxiii.
Of the cure of the dislocation of the foot.
The foot is sometimes reduced by slight movement, and sometimes dislocated by force, and the dislocation of the foot is done either internally or externally.
And the sign of the dislocation of the foot is that you see the foot swollen proceeding from the part to which it is dislocated.
More the cure of the reduction of the foot is by gentle restoration, and it is that it be extended with ease and equalized so that it returns.
More in the cure of the foot when it is dislocated by force, make sure you straighten the limb properly, and have a strong and brave assistant behind the injured part in the middle of that area.
And then place your right hand on the foot of the upper part, and your left hand on the lower part of the foot, placing your hand in the location of the heel.
And then pull the foot to your right hand, and then to your left hand twice, and then extend your left hand; and push the foot to the right with the leg bent on the left.
And do this twice as we have counted.
And then push the foot to the leg for the third time, and you are at the heel.
So if it returns in one or two pushes using this method, and if you grasp the foot evenly, that is good; otherwise, then iterate the operation, as it may not return.
But if the reduction is forbidden to you as we have advised, then place the foot in a basin on the ground, and insert a wooden stick into the ground firmly, and let it sit between the patient's thighs, and you have already wrapped it with a cloth to prevent it from moving.
And then the minister hold their thighs, and another minister extend the foot with their hands or with a stick placed on the back of the foot; and then each minister extends against the length of their part, and the wooden stick remains between the patient's thighs holding it there, so that the body of the patient is not pulled towards the sides you release its extension.
And then the doctor equalizes the dislocation with his hands, while another assistant holds the healthy thigh at the parts left, so that the dislocation returns with ease.
And then as the dislocation returns and its reduction is declared to you, then pause and apply a strong paste externally there, and bind the foot with linen to the leg.
And be sure to avoid or guard against the nerves behind the talus, lest the linen over that area be too strong and severely affect those nerves.
And then leave that alone for two or three days; and if the linen becomes moist, apply it externally.
And then remove the linen on the third day or fourth, and prohibit the patient from walking for forty days. But if it is necessary for the patient to go before that time, be sure that the dislocation is not completely dissolved and destroyed, and that it does not receive treatment.
And if an abscess occurs in him, then cover the place where you administer as we described in our narrative in another location for healing and fomentation, until it is ripe.
Capitol.xxxiv.
For the treatment of dislocation of the toes of the feet.
Cover that which is dislocated so that it is gently extended a little; what is not difficult, beforehand is easier.
More if the dislocation is in some joints of the big toe, then cover that you place the foot on a level part of the ground or on a table, and let it be thus as if walking.
Then stand on your foot, and place your foot over the area near the joints, and press that part with your foot to apply pressure until it returns, and observe that which is gently extended and does not appear as a prominence in the area.
Then place the abdomen of his foot on a table that can hold his entire foot, which has two supports, and firmly strengthen it with a stable structure for three days; then loosen it and allow him to rest by walking for several days until it is strengthened and its recovery is assured.
Capitol.xxxv.
Of the types of dislocation that occur from a blow or from a fracture or any other combination.
When any of these causes occur, and you are involved in the treatment and restoration of it, mistakes can lead to death.
And for this reason, you must not presume in the treatment of it, but only someone who is skilled in the art, long practiced, merciful, and pious; who delays, is not rash, nor presumptuous; and who uses at the beginning of medicines that can reduce abscesses by cautery alone, leaving the disease to divine judgment, rather than to him to which you hope for recovery from illness, in alleviation of the disease, and in which any hope appears. And then strive to perform the reduction at the beginning of the cause, before it develops into an abscess.
And then, if the limb returns as we wish, administer a regimen that reduces the abscess by cautery and treats the blow with what is suitable for it from the drying ointments.
And if dislocation or fracture occurs in the separated bones of the thigh, then you must reduce their displacement, and do so as we have said in simple ailments that precede these in the places mentioned above.
And guard and avoid according to your virtue that of the instrument in the ways of fear according to which my preaching precedes you; for this is better to you from your Creator, and more permanent to your glory, and more greetings to your true soul those in your time. Thus you finish the words of Albucasim perfectly of which may God bless. Amen.
